<div id="review_418841091" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/418841091" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6803829-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944642p2/6803829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/418841091?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/6803829-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061969669" href="/book/show/13623969-death-of-kings">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=middle-ages">middle-ages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer418841091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7347693530382578067">So yeah, these are still good. I've pretty much run out of ways to say "these books are still good" in the beginning of these reviews without actually saying it so I figured I'd just actually say it. This one was a bit different in vibe from the others, though. It's hard to pin down exactly what made it stand out from the other so much. It seemed darker, mostly due to the whole Alfred thing. Also the whole "cave witch" scene rivaled the talking corpse from <i>Sword Song</i> in terms of creepiest scenes</span>
  <span id="freeText7347693530382578067" style="display:none">So yeah, these are still good. I've pretty much run out of ways to say "these books are still good" in the beginning of these reviews without actually saying it so I figured I'd just actually say it. This one was a bit different in vibe from the others, though. It's hard to pin down exactly what made it stand out from the other so much. It seemed darker, mostly due to the whole Alfred thing. Also the whole "cave witch" scene rivaled the talking corpse from <i>Sword Song</i> in terms of creepiest scenes having to do with "magic" from this series. It's easy to look at these things as a 21st-century person and see them as the obvious, cheesy human trickery that it is, but on the other hand I'm sure it seemed very real and terrifying to the people at the time and I think Cornwell captures that well.<br /><br />The action of the story was a slight change of pace, it seemed almost more in line with <i>Lords of the North</i> than one of the novels like <i>The Pale Horseman</i> or <i>The Burning Land</i>. It has more to do with mysteries, plots and intrigue that Uhtred is confronted with and how he goes about dealing with it rather than big history-making battles (although Cornwell does have his usual scrap at the end.) The struggle of the story is more a mental one involving deception and sketchy stuff like that rather than one of physical conflict.<br /><br />You just have to like Uhtred at this point. Throughout the novel he's constantly out-thinking his enemies, publicly shaming people for hypocrisy and general douchery, committing hilarious acts of blasphemous counterintelligence and displaying a fierce loyalty to the Saxons. For all of the back-and-forth he went through in the other novels between the Danes and Saxons it's clear where his loyalty is at this point and it's refreshing. He almost comes off as warm and genial in some scenes, although he's still the kind of guy that hangs people and stuff. In general he's a big difference from the guy in <i>The Last Kingdom</i> and <i>The Pale Horseman</i>, which I think shows a deeper characterization than I think a lot of people give Cornwell credit for.<br /><br />Well, I'm finally caught up with this series. I haven't been disappointed yet and I'm looking forward to the next one in the series. There are several loose ends that need to be wrapped including the obvious big one and I definitely need to see those. At some point in the near future I'm going to start Cornwell's Arthur stuff and I'm excited to read a different series by this author and see how it strikes me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7347693530382578067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_296640430" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/296640430?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/1528196-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780061969652" href="/book/show/11940907-death-of-kings">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1528196-joe?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer296640430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16429946214662292583">Ah, another historical action novels from one of my guilty pleasures, Bernard Cornwell. I know, it's not literature but it sure is fun. I've heard him called the greatest living historical novelist. That might be true if we insist on the "living" qualification. While his books are always grand action-adventures of male fantasy, he's not quite Patrick O'Brian.<br/><br/>This latest addition to the chronical of Uhtred, the Saxon warlord is a delightful departure from the usual Cornwell style. The grand, spla</span>
  <span id="freeText16429946214662292583" style="display:none">Ah, another historical action novels from one of my guilty pleasures, Bernard Cornwell. I know, it's not literature but it sure is fun. I've heard him called the greatest living historical novelist. That might be true if we insist on the "living" qualification. While his books are always grand action-adventures of male fantasy, he's not quite Patrick O'Brian.<br /><br />This latest addition to the chronical of Uhtred, the Saxon warlord is a delightful departure from the usual Cornwell style. The grand, splattery battle scene (and there is always a grand splattery, battle scene) is held back until the end. Uhtred, no passing into middle age, sees his fortunes rise with the passing of Alfred, the king who has so often manipulated him into service against his will. Though with the king's passing Uhtred's heart changes as he becomes a willing servant Alfred's dream of a united England.<br /><br />Through much of the novel we see Uhtred engaged in a campaign of trickery and deception against many of his Danish foes. To say too much risks giving spoilers. I'll say only that his schemes are inspired by a rather fun rogue of a character, Ludda, a wandering scam artist, and abetted Cuthbert, a likable priest of easy principals. It's quite a departure from the usual brooding gloom of most Cornwell heroes, and something Cornwell should do more often. Quite refreshing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16429946214662292583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_246914166" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/246914166" />
    <a title="Kate Quinn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2232084-kate-quinn"><img alt="Kate Quinn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534961434p2/2232084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/246914166?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate Quinn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate Quinn" href="/user/show/2232084-kate-quinn">Kate Quinn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer246914166" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15462098982741453248">The sixth installment in the adventures of Uhtred of Bebbanburg, impatiently awaited and worth the wait. This one has a more thoughtful tone than the others, since it hinges on everyone waiting to see when Alfred the Great will die, and whether they will be able to survive the attack that will surely come from the Danes afterward. Their best hope of keeping Alfred's fragile kingdom together is Uhtred, but will Alfred's young successor trust him? Uhtred snaps and snarls and swears at priests, del</span>
  <span id="freeText15462098982741453248" style="display:none">The sixth installment in the adventures of Uhtred of Bebbanburg, impatiently awaited and worth the wait. This one has a more thoughtful tone than the others, since it hinges on everyone waiting to see when Alfred the Great will die, and whether they will be able to survive the attack that will surely come from the Danes afterward. Their best hope of keeping Alfred's fragile kingdom together is Uhtred, but will Alfred's young successor trust him? Uhtred snaps and snarls and swears at priests, delightfully crochety as ever, but Alfred's impending death and his own advancing years (he is startled to find his beard gray, and has to admit that at forty-five he is officially old) have given Uhtred a more elegiac bent. Old or not, Uhtred can still swing and chop in the shield wall with the best of them, and Cornwell's battle scenes are as good as ever. Alfred's daughter Aethelflaed is a welcome sidekick, the only woman with enough steel in her spine to keep up with Uhtred, and the subplot with Uhtred's children is growing more interesting by the book. A strong chapter in the tales of Uhtred, though I have to wonder if he's ever going to go north and retake his castle as he's been threatening since Book 1?</span>
  <a data-text-id="15462098982741453248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2030267095803930327">Uhtred is back. Without giving away plot spoilers though, the book occupies awkward territory. We know it's the penultimate book in the series and the entire novel reads like a build up to that climax. Which it is; a cruel trick to play on us readers and I can only hope that the final volume arrives a little more quickly than this episode did.<br/><br/>Uhtred has some fun along the way, but is mostly chasing shadows through the book, and it's always a little disappointing to figure things out before the h</span>
  <span id="freeText2030267095803930327" style="display:none">Uhtred is back. Without giving away plot spoilers though, the book occupies awkward territory. We know it's the penultimate book in the series and the entire novel reads like a build up to that climax. Which it is; a cruel trick to play on us readers and I can only hope that the final volume arrives a little more quickly than this episode did.<br /><br />Uhtred has some fun along the way, but is mostly chasing shadows through the book, and it's always a little disappointing to figure things out before the hero. That said, it kept me gripped from beginning to end and, as I said, leaves me even more impatient for the conclusion. That, surely, is the hallmark of a great story? One that you want to know the ending to, but don't want to end.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer713989426" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer669739313696810284"><b>4 Stars</b> for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11734251.Death_of_Kings__The_Saxon_Stories___6_' title='Death of Kings (The Saxon Stories, #6) by Bernard Cornwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Death of Kings</a>. Uhtred is back in form after the last 2 books on the series where I thought he just meandered aimlessly. Uhtred is the man with a plan in this chapter. Alfred finally kicks the bucket--after so many episodes on the "death bed". Seriously, Alfred was like a parody of a Shakespeare play, he's dead....no he's well...he's dead...no he's alive... Edward becomes king but can he keep the crown? Some great new characters show up here and Cornwell's treatment of Christians and</span>
  <span id="freeText669739313696810284" style="display:none"><b>4 Stars</b> for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11734251.Death_of_Kings__The_Saxon_Stories___6_" title="Death of Kings (The Saxon Stories, #6) by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">Death of Kings</a>. Uhtred is back in form after the last 2 books on the series where I thought he just meandered aimlessly. Uhtred is the man with a plan in this chapter. Alfred finally kicks the bucket--after so many episodes on the "death bed". Seriously, Alfred was like a parody of a Shakespeare play, he's dead....no he's well...he's dead...no he's alive... Edward becomes king but can he keep the crown? Some great new characters show up here and Cornwell's treatment of Christians and priests is more complex than before. This period in English history really comes alive under Cornwell's hand. Recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="669739313696810284" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Except our true hero, Uhtred, who saves them yet again. ‘The Saxon Stories’ continues to impress. Absolutely love the setting, the characters, and the intrigue. Cornwell shows his mastery of British history and of the medieval arts of war. He is also an able and careful writer and leaves no loose ends. What else can I say about these books that I haven’t already? </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12223064617518230781">I LOVE this series. Really! A lot! This book is the 6th one in the Saxon Stories series by Bernard Cornwell. I love the way this author writes the story and how he stretches out his characters. I have enjoyed seeing Uhtred grow up. <br/><br/>This book had a lot of death and war in it. So did other books in the series, but this one seemed like it was all about that one thing. I missed Uhtred's antics as well as those of King Alfred.   <span id="freeText12223064617518230781" style="display:none">I LOVE this series. Really! A lot! This book is the 6th one in the Saxon Stories series by Bernard Cornwell. I love the way this author writes the story and how he stretches out his characters. I have enjoyed seeing Uhtred grow up. <br /><br />This book had a lot of death and war in it. So did other books in the series, but this one seemed like it was all about that one thing. I missed Uhtred's antics as well as those of King Alfred. Basically, I missed the "relationships" in this one. I've also recognized that almost every book has naked women in it. This one had more than a few. While I'd generally roll my eyes because of this constant re-purposed detail, I still am enjoying this series. I can't wait to get to the next one. So 4 stars.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10100971243875072887">Enjoyed being in the Saxon world again -- although my time spent there does make me appreciate 21st century, even with all its current problems, more. <br/><br/>King Alfred is dying as the book opens and no one is sure if his expanded kingdom of Wessex will continue to be able to fight off Danish invaders.   <span id="freeText10100971243875072887" style="display:none">Enjoyed being in the Saxon world again -- although my time spent there does make me appreciate 21st century, even with all its current problems, more. <br /><br />King Alfred is dying as the book opens and no one is sure if his expanded kingdom of Wessex will continue to be able to fight off Danish invaders. I enjoy imagining the landscape of England in those days with its many Roman ruins and the forts and stones of the "Old Ones". The Saxons build their thatch roofed halls and churches and watch them go up in flames as the Danes keep trying to take over the country. But now Alfred's son, Edward, relies again on Uhtred and his battle skills and scheming to keep the Saxons and London safe through a few more years. King Alfred's daughter, AEthelflaed, is finally coming into her own power in Mercia, the area adjoining Wessex, and we get to see a strong female historical character. <br /><br />I may take a break from the series now but I expect to keep going . . . I think there are 12 in this series and this one is only #6. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10100971243875072887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_225820061" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/225820061" />
    <a title="Willow " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4529669-willow"><img alt="Willow " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472180488p2/4529669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/225820061?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Willow " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Willow " href="/user/show/4529669-willow">Willow </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4529669-willow?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4529669-willow?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4529669-willow?shelf=viking">viking</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225820061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17995288946092068285">I decided to give “Death of Kings” five stars, simply because I was just so happy to read about Uthred again. What can I say, I just love his character. I must admit though, this book isn’t as good as Pale Horseman by a long shot. But of course, that one was simply my fav. This book seemed to end somewhat abruptly, so I can see where some reviewers might have felt cheated. <br/><br/>There are so many things I love about Bernard Cornwell’s writing. One is that he captures the time period so well. I feel li</span>
  <span id="freeText17995288946092068285" style="display:none">I decided to give “Death of Kings” five stars, simply because I was just so happy to read about Uthred again. What can I say, I just love his character. I must admit though, this book isn’t as good as Pale Horseman by a long shot. But of course, that one was simply my fav. This book seemed to end somewhat abruptly, so I can see where some reviewers might have felt cheated. <br /><br />There are so many things I love about Bernard Cornwell’s writing. One is that he captures the time period so well. I feel like I’m there, in the tenth century. He also knows how to capture the medieval mind. Uttred is not a modern person. He would definitely be shunned in today’s society, but with the life and times he lives in, he seems to fit perfectly into his element. <br /><br />Things I’ve read that people don’t like about Cornwell’s writing is that he somewhat slams the church. But I don’t think that’s necessarily true. Let’s face it, the church was a power to be reckoned with during this time, and Cornwell certainly doesn’t shy away from the more insidious abuses that happened. Yet not everyone in the church is a pain in the ass. There are some good priests, and Uthred even makes a concession, deciding that part of the reason Alfred was a great king was his unshakable faith. <br /><br />Another criticism of Cornwell’s writing is that Uthred almost always wins every battle and gets to kill the bad guys. But I personally find that very satisfying. One thing that I really liked about this book was that Alfred finally acknowledged how import Uthred was too him, and rewarded him for his service. I was getting so tired of Uhtred winning all these battles and then being attacked and insulted by priests and Alfred treating him with disdain. .<br /><br />I will admit that Cornwell’s female characters are almost always lacking and are certainly not as rich and full blooded as his male characters. But that is a flaw I’ve decided to overlook, because so many of his other characters are so rich. <br /><br />So all in all, I loved this book. I am definitely looking forward to book eight. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17995288946092068285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_235878658" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/235878658" />
    <a title="Rob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/946048-rob"><img alt="Rob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395086419p2/946048.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/235878658?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob" href="/user/show/946048-rob">Rob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer235878658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11231384101343132562">Once again, Bernard Cornwell has provided me with a page turner that simply cannot be put down. Uhtred is probably one of my favourite anti-heroes, with his gruff demeanor, love of battle and hatred and disdain for the church, whats not to love? <br/><br/>As always, Bernards story resides within historical fact, with the death of King Alfred and the succession of his son King Edward the Elder eventually making battle with the Danes, with the backdrop of Aethelwolds revolt included. I love these books beca</span>
  <span id="freeText11231384101343132562" style="display:none">Once again, Bernard Cornwell has provided me with a page turner that simply cannot be put down. Uhtred is probably one of my favourite anti-heroes, with his gruff demeanor, love of battle and hatred and disdain for the church, whats not to love? <br /><br />As always, Bernards story resides within historical fact, with the death of King Alfred and the succession of his son King Edward the Elder eventually making battle with the Danes, with the backdrop of Aethelwolds revolt included. I love these books because they follow true historical events so closely, many of the characters being interpretations of people that lived and died at the time.<br /><br />Uhtreds personal story has not advanced too much with this book, Bebbanburg still remains just out of reach, but it was nice to see on Alfreds Death, that Uhtred finally got a worthy reward from the previously, rather scroogey king. <br /><br />With the large land holdings he now commands, it should make raising men and money that much easier, so with Uhtred getting a bit long in the tooth, hopefully we will see him strike out towards his birth rite in the next book.<br /><br />As with all of these books, there is plenty of the ol Ultra Violence, so not a read for those faint of heart... ;)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11231384101343132562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1285508938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5870290063120962800">Uhtred is old, and he doesn't know why he's ended up where he has. Uhtred is in love with the dying King's married daughter. Uhtred is clever, and a good strategist. Uhtred stabs a bunch of people in the face, neck, thigh, groin. Uhtred gets rained on. Uhtred's house burns down. Uhtred profanes a nunnery. Uhtred expresses skepticism at the provenance of saintly relics. Uhtred thinks his son is a pansy.<br/><br/>Recommended for anyone interested in Uhtred.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2NC9NVWHIDHIeYO8UpCygaayskPxY/zYV9q9dGKCpexyKkEYV5d6cA8XPnt4TroZsnH0A6z4vcoTOL2HJjksmw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('34rrKi+1BY2R8F59Nc6QTyIzConC9o3rSTp4Z5tCD+t1cBcHLaVfzFae47ofEJjXNvBMyZ9tzPkN2HiU3/mGnA==')})
  }

What emerges is both a grand romantic tale of coming-of-age in the Roman Empire and a vibrant portrait of the early years of a man who would become the most powerful ruler on earth: Julius Caesar. Against this vivid backdrop, two boys are growing to manhood, dreaming of battles, fame, and glory in service of the mightiest empire the world has ever known. One is the son of a senator, a boy of privilege and ambition to whom much has been given and from whom much is expected. The other is a bastard child, a boy of strength and cunning, whose love for his adoptive family-and his adoptive brother-will be the most powerful force in his life. As young Gaius and Marcus are trained in the art of combat-under the tutelage of one of Rome\'s most fearsome gladiators-Rome itself is being rocked by the art of treachery and ambition, caught in a tug-of-war as two rival generals, Marius and Sulla, push the empire toward civil war. For Marcus, a bloody campaign in Greece will become a young soldier\'s proving ground. For Gaius, the equally deadly infighting of the Roman Senate will be the battlefield where he hones his courage and skill. And for both, the love of an extraordinary slave girl will be an honor each will covet but only one will win. The two friends are forced to walk different paths, and by the time they meet again everything will have changed. Both will have known love, loss, and violence. And the land where they were once innocent will be thrust into the grip of bitter conflict-a conflict that will set Roman against Roman...and put their friendship to the ultimate test. Brilliantly interweaving history and adventure, Conn Iggulden conjures a stunning array of contrasts-from the bloody stench of a battlefield to the opulence of the greatest city in history, from the tenderness of a lover to the treachery of an assassin. Here, in a series of cataclysmic clashes, the legend of Julius Caesar will be forged. And while Caesar and Brutus pit their lives–and those of their men–against the armies of the wilderness, their political adversaries in Rome grow at once more fearful and more formidable. So when the fighting at the dominion’s edge is over, the greatest danger to Julius Caesar will await him on the Tiber–with a man who wants Rome himself. From the clash of armies to the heat of a woman’s seduction, from the thunder of battle to the orgies of pleasure and plunder that follow in a warrior’s wake, Emperor: The Field of Swords captures in riveting detail a world being shaped by a brilliant civilization.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">About Bernard Cornwell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1504578807p3/12542.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">Bernard Cornwell
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
13,027
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17002153581032326519">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his birth mother's maiden n</span>
  <span id="freeText17002153581032326519" style="display:none">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his birth mother's maiden name, Cornwell.<br /><br />Cornwell was sent away to Monkton Combe School, attended the University of London, and after graduating, worked as a teacher. He attempted to enlist in the British armed services at least three times but was rejected on the grounds of myopia.<br /><br />He then joined BBC's Nationwide and was promoted to become head of current affairs at BBC Northern Ireland. He then joined Thames Television as editor of Thames News. He relocated to the United States in 1980 after marrying an American. Unable to get a green card, he started writing novels, as this did not require a work permit.<br /><br />As a child, Cornwell loved the novels of C.S. Forester, chronicling the adventures of fictional British naval officer Horatio Hornblower during the Napoleonic Wars, and was surprised to find there were no such novels following Lord Wellington's campaign on land. Motivated by the need to support himself in the U.S. through writing, Cornwell decided to write such a series. He named his chief protagonist Richard Sharpe, a rifleman involved in most major battles of the Peninsular War.<br /><br />Cornwell wanted to start the series with the Siege of Badajoz but decided instead to start with a couple of "warm-up" novels. These were <i>Sharpe's Eagle</i> and <i>Sharpe's Gold</i>, both published in 1981. <i>Sharpe's Eagle</i> was picked up by a publisher, and Cornwell got a three-book deal. He went on to tell the story of Badajoz in his third Sharpe novel, <i>Sharpe's Company</i>, published in 1982.<br /><br />Cornwell and wife Judy co-wrote a series of novels, published under the pseudonym "Susannah Kells". These were <i>A Crowning Mercy</i>, published in 1983, <i>Fallen Angels</i> in 1984, and <i>Coat of Arms</i> (aka <i>The Aristocrats</i>) in 1986. (Cornwell's strict Protestant upbringing informed the background of <i>A Crowning Mercy</i>, which took place during the English Civil War.) In 1987, he also published <i>Redcoat</i>, an American Revolutionary War novel set in Philadelphia during its 1777 occupation by the British.<br /><br />After publishing eight books in his ongoing Sharpe series, Cornwell was approached by a production company interested in adapting them for television. The producers asked him to write a prequel to give them a starting point to the series. They also requested that the story feature a large role for Spanish characters to secure co-funding from Spain. The result was <i>Sharpe’s Rifles</i>, published in 1987, and a series of Sharpe television films staring Sean Bean.<br /><br />A series of contemporary thrillers with sailing as a background and common themes followed: <i>Wildtrack</i> published in 1988, <i>Sea Lord</i> (aka <i>Killer's Wake</i>) in 1989, <i>Crackdown</i> in 1990, <i>Stormchild</i> in 1991, and <i>Scoundrel</i>, a political thriller, in 1992.<br /><br />In June 2006, Cornwell was made an Officer of the Order of the British Empire in the Queen's 80th Birthday Honours List.<br /><br />Cornwell's latest work, <i>Azincourt</i>, was released in the UK in October 2008. The protagonist is an archer who participates in the Battle of Agincourt, another devastating defeat suffered by the French in the Hundred Years War. However, Cornwell has stated that it will not be about Thomas of Hookton from The Grail Quest or any of his relatives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17002153581032326519" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

The old enemy comes from Wessex where a dynastic struggle will determine who will be the next king.  And the new foe is Sköll, a Norseman, whose ambition is to be King of Northumbria and who leads a frightening army of wolf-warriors, men who fight half-crazed in the belief that they are indeed wolves. Uhtred, believing he is cursed, must fend off one enemy while he tries to destroy the other. In this new chapter of the Saxon Tales series—a rousing adventure of courage, treachery, duty, devotion, majesty, love and battle, as seen through the eyes of a warrior straddling two worlds—Uhtred
